<div id="review_70706030" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/70706030" />
    <a title="Chloe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70078-chloe"><img alt="Chloe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580564408p2/70078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/70706030?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chloe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chloe" href="/user/show/70078-chloe">Chloe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Chloe by: </span>
      Lorena K.
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=not-owned">not-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=library_book">library_book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70706030" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14540919099839211935">Everybody loves a hero. Everybody loves it even more when a hero falls from grace. There are few things that humans enjoy more than taking a powerful person down a peg or two. In fact, we get a sick thrill from it. Whether it’s the rising up of a virginal starlet (take your pick, they’re a dime a dozen) so that we may delight in tearing her to pieces when she is unable to live up to the exceptionally demanding standards of behavior we set for <i>others</i> to abide by, or the fall from grace of an espe</span>
  <span id="freeText14540919099839211935" style="display:none">Everybody loves a hero. Everybody loves it even more when a hero falls from grace. There are few things that humans enjoy more than taking a powerful person down a peg or two. In fact, we get a sick thrill from it. Whether it’s the rising up of a virginal starlet (take your pick, they’re a dime a dozen) so that we may delight in tearing her to pieces when she is unable to live up to the exceptionally demanding standards of behavior we set for <i>others</i> to abide by, or the fall from grace of an especially sanctimonious politician (again, take your pick), these ritualistic sacrifices of character seem to sate an intrinsic need for blood-letting. I guess this should not be too surprising when we stop to consider that one of the major world faiths is predicated on humans killing their own Redeemer. Verily, the schadenfreude runs deep in our culture. <br /> <br />I had no idea what to expect when I opened <i>The General In His Labyrinth</i>. All I knew was that it was written by Colombia’s third most famous export, the eminent Gabriel Garcia Marquez, (Shakira can suck it, she’s #4) and was a paean to the man who wrested South America from the hands of its Spanish Colonial masters, Simon Bolivar. I figured it would be a delightful romp through military campaigns and political skullduggery as reflected through the always enjoyable lens of Garcia Marquez’s magical realist style. Not so much.<br /> <br /><i>The General In His Labyrinth</i> finds the El Libertador at the end of his life. Tired and weary after over two decades of continuous war, Bolivar is a husk of a man, not the proud and full-chested figure represented in countless statues throughout Latin America. A piñata battered again and again by foes and former friends alike, Bolivar is in the unenviable position of seeing himself become a living anachronism as his nation, the country that he has shed so much blood for, outgrow its need for him. Meticulously researched from the memoirs of his confidants and his last surviving records, Garcia Marquez fairly drowns the reader in mundane details of the General’s day to day life as he prepares to either go into exile, return to power by a coup, or die. These may seem like contradictory goals to the outsider but, as the General’s aide repeats, “only my master knows what my master is thinking.”<br /><br />As the weary band of warriors makes its way toward the coast, we are treated to a backwards look at Bolivar’s life as each village he passes through or compatriot who joins him on the road kindles the memory of his previous glory. The insult hurled at Bolivar by a little boy conjures memories of his first abdication of power, a slave girl reminds him of a tumultuous night between the sheets of another lover long left behind, the tolling of church bells reminds of the time when Bolivar first freed a town from its Colonial masters. <br /><br />The descriptions, as they always are in Garcia Marquez’s books, are lush and beautiful, though there are none of the flights of fancy that one could expect from his regular fiction. Here he seems, at times, a bit too chained to the historical veracity of his subject to let much whimsy into the story. However, forcing him to work within a framework like this makes those moments when he gets to cut loose all the more enjoyable, whether he’s describing the ruins of Cartagena or the banks of a river. This wouldn’t be the book I recommend to those new to Gabriel Garcia Marquez as an author, though anyone who is interested in either Latin American history will find this an interesting look at the last days of the Colombian George Washington, the man who freed half of a continent from its Spanish masters. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14540919099839211935" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2163520368" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2163520368" />
    <a title="Hoda Marmar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3655700-hoda-marmar"><img alt="Hoda Marmar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572216889p2/3655700.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2163520368?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hoda Marmar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hoda Marmar" href="/user/show/3655700-hoda-marmar">Hoda Marmar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789953593180" href="/book/show/42119261">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=12genres">12genres</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=bookoholics">bookoholics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=arabic">arabic</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2163520368" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7742267916827684888">Marquez' The General in His Labyrinth is definitely worthy of a second and third read. Where do I begin to tell about this unusual adventure with this book?<br/><br/>1. THE Title. <br/>I love the title, I love that it is based on what Bolivar actually said on his death bed, I love the lingering feeling it evokes, and I love how it glues the whole narrative together for the general is lost in a labyrinth he doesn't seem to free himself from since the first and until the last page of this novel. <br/>“Damn it,' he si</span>
  <span id="freeText7742267916827684888" style="display:none">Marquez' The General in His Labyrinth is definitely worthy of a second and third read. Where do I begin to tell about this unusual adventure with this book?<br /><br />1. THE Title. <br />I love the title, I love that it is based on what Bolivar actually said on his death bed, I love the lingering feeling it evokes, and I love how it glues the whole narrative together for the general is lost in a labyrinth he doesn't seem to free himself from since the first and until the last page of this novel. <br />“Damn it,' he sighed. 'How will I ever get out of this labyrinth!”<br /><br />2. "Nobody loves us here." <br />Marquez argues that the general's illness is not physical but rather a sickness of the heart, a lack of love, and that is what kills him: that loveless labyrinth he is stuck in. He fails at love, because: <br />“You're a great man, General, greater than anyone," she told him. "But love is still too big for you.”<br />His relationship with women is tightly related to and springs from his Bonaparte-like eccentric personality. He runs away from love and fears the entanglements of falling in love which is “like having two souls at the same time.” <br /><br />3. The element of water.<br />Latin America, this continent floating in the Pacific Ocean is made up of countries and territories that the general fought for uniting - or so he believes -. On a smaller scale, these waves of war and poverty and revolution wash over each territory and each citizen. “America is half a world gone mad.”<br />Mud, rains, orphans' and widows' tears, fog, heat, purifying waters, hot infusions, sweat, storms, and water beds are symbols of what is facing the general, the country, the citizens, and the political scene. The statements made by the general describe both what is literally and what is generally happening. Many spoken lines are quotable pieces of wit and sarcasm such as “The only wars here will be civil wars, and those are like killing your own mother.”<br />This fluidity further touches on the telling of memories and flashbacks and overall bits of stories and histories in the novel making it an un-put-down-able read for the reader. Though, I admit it, this novel isn't for everyone; it is a magical realism take on Bolivar's biography which includes a lot of characters and settings (both time and place).<br /><br />4. The general's personality.<br />This sarcastic, mean, kind, scared, defensive, choleric, cynical, exhibitionist, dark humored, prophetic, controlling man in denial describes himself as being “old, sick, tired, disillusioned, harassed, slandered, and unappreciated... I'm at the mercy of a destiny that isn't mine.”<br /><br />5. The characters' dynamics.<br />I really loved the complexity in the characters' dynamics, especially between the general and José Palacios, Manuela, de Sucre, and Santander which symbolize respectively the themes of loyalty/friendship, love/jealousy, power/love, power/rivalry.<br /><br />6. The highly quotable material!<br />There are so many awesome quotes in this book. Though I really struggled with the endless characters and setting shifts, but I really focused on and let myself be absorbed by the spirit of the novel and its many powerful quotes. One of my favorites is: “There is great power in the irresistible force of love.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="7742267916827684888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_676060057" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/676060057" />
    <a title="Louise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9281934-louise"><img alt="Louise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336194494p2/9281934.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/676060057?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Louise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Louise" href="/user/show/9281934-louise">Louise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9281934-louise?shelf=biographical-fiction">biographical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9281934-louise?shelf=south-america-fic-lit">south-america-fic-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9281934-louise?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9281934-louise?shelf=nobel-prize-winning-author">nobel-prize-winning-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer676060057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1043825018122952796">This book is not a work of magical realism; Neither is it an insightful biography. There are flashes of Marquez brilliance (for instance, the prose in the ending), but, overall, this book left me wanting.<br/><br/>I tried to read it several years ago, but didn't have the background. To prepare myself I started a number of Bolivar biographies, but didn't have the background for them either. I recently found what I needed in Marie Arana's "Bolivar: American Liberator" where I learned about Francisco Miranda</span>
  <span id="freeText1043825018122952796" style="display:none">This book is not a work of magical realism; Neither is it an insightful biography. There are flashes of Marquez brilliance (for instance, the prose in the ending), but, overall, this book left me wanting.<br /><br />I tried to read it several years ago, but didn't have the background. To prepare myself I started a number of Bolivar biographies, but didn't have the background for them either. I recently found what I needed in Marie Arana's "Bolivar: American Liberator" where I learned about Francisco Miranda, Francisco de Paula Santander, Jose Antonio Paez, Manuel Saenz, Daniel O'Leary and the others who appear here. I learned that Bolivar was once among the richest men in all of South America and I learned of his parents and their passing in his young life. I learned of his travels, his tutors, and his marriage. I also learned about his heroism and how he became disillusioned, viewing the Revolution he led as "plowing the sea" and how he saw his life's end as a labyrinth.<br /><br />This book is a novelization of the final months of Bolivar's life. He is traveling on the on the Magdalena River, a river from which he drove out the royalists and was acclaimed. As he travels, the cheering crowds are smaller; He sees the destruction and poverty that the war brought. While the enemy has been vanquished, former friends are now enemies. His entourage is not sure of the destination or what his plan might be. His resources are diminishing and he does not have a passport (until the end, and then... maybe not). He is sad, cynical but not fully resigned to his labyrinth and illness; There are times he is ready to jump back in the fray (he talks of starting over in Venezuela), but he is mostly sad and very sick.<br /><br />All the elements of the story are here, but missing is a core message about Bolivar. The battles, the presidencies, the decisions and turning points, the betrayals and ironies of his life devolve into clever quips. With each page Bolivar's illness physically shrinks him such that he should have disappeared by p. 200. He expresses himself through ironic one liners. The full measure of his life experience and the complexity of his character do not show through.<br /><br />The fascinating characters, their loyalties and deceit, are no more memorable from this narrative than is the General. Dramatic episodes of Bolivar's life are presented in short, flat anecdotes. The many ironies of his life are distilled into pithy ironic comments.<br /><br />Marquez has done his homework and the reporting is good, but this is a novel. The General and his sad labyrinth do not come alive for the reader. Neither the character nor situation resonate. This powerful story calls out for a better telling either in the magical realism genre for which Marquez is a master or a narrative biography that helps the readers understand and feel the tragedy of the Liberator's last days.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1043825018122952796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1245588890" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1245588890" />
    <a title="Shawn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11011728-shawn"><img alt="Shawn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442516511p2/11011728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1245588890?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shawn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shawn" href="/user/show/11011728-shawn">Shawn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394582580" href="/book/show/426090.The_General_in_His_Labyrinth">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1245588890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5142714844228381331">I wavered between a three and four star on this classic but finally settled on three stars. Perhaps it lost something in the translation from Spanish to English but I had a tough time following the narrative. The novel depicts the final voyage of the great Latin American liberator, General Simon Bolivar, down the Magdalena River in 1830. The general has fallen from power and his dreams of a united South American nation-state have deteriorated following the wars of liberation. The book is sort of</span>
  <span id="freeText5142714844228381331" style="display:none">I wavered between a three and four star on this classic but finally settled on three stars. Perhaps it lost something in the translation from Spanish to English but I had a tough time following the narrative. The novel depicts the final voyage of the great Latin American liberator, General Simon Bolivar, down the Magdalena River in 1830. The general has fallen from power and his dreams of a united South American nation-state have deteriorated following the wars of liberation. The book is sort of a stream of consciousness novel, yet it also describes the thoughts and motives of those surrounding the general. I really did not get a true sense of the proper point of view until I decided, half-way through the book, the work was not intended to give the reader a clear picture.<br />The imagery and word selection was brilliant and masterful but still there was something missing. I often pick up historical novels involving regions and time frames that I know little about hoping to get pick up a sense of time and place. I did not get that with this novel. In the beginning of the book and the general's journey, the word to describe the winds in Bogata was "icy". Does it ever get icy in Bogata, Columbia?<br />Perhaps the factor that bothered me the most was that General Bolivar was not that likeable or even remarkable a character. His relationships with his underlings were shallow and one-sided and his interactions with women even worse. I did not get a sense what could have inspired such loyalty and fear from those around him other than his prognostication early during the wars that the rebels would rout the Spanish.<br />The map in the front of the page and chronology of Bolivar's life at the end were very helpful since I do know so very little about the geography and history of Columbia, Venezuela, and Peru. Without them I would have been completely lost. The general's mind constantly wanders back to past glories and conquests, both on the battlefield and the bedroom. I wish I knew more about the personalities and the politics of the period and I feel I should have learned more from this book.<br />Still the language and thoughts and beautiful and profound. Perhaps, I should have read it as a grand poem rather than a limited biography of Bolivar. It was a good book and I will read more of this amazing Colombian author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5142714844228381331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_96964731" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/96964731" />
    <a title="Rick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/764994-rick"><img alt="Rick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289746453p2/764994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/96964731?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rick" href="/user/show/764994-rick">Rick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140245295" href="/book/show/602387.The_General_in_His_Labyrinth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/764994-rick?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96964731" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11009358052738587997">Garcia-Marquez, one of the world’s literary masters, not just of this era but of all time, tells the story of Simon Bolivar’s last months in this thoughtful, moving, elegiac novel. Bolivar is the Great Liberator, freeing the South American holdings of Spain from imperial rule, intending to create a single federalist republic of the former colonial states, a United States of South America, but having won independence, there are squabbles and rebellions among the armies of liberation. Military and</span>
  <span id="freeText11009358052738587997" style="display:none">Garcia-Marquez, one of the world’s literary masters, not just of this era but of all time, tells the story of Simon Bolivar’s last months in this thoughtful, moving, elegiac novel. Bolivar is the Great Liberator, freeing the South American holdings of Spain from imperial rule, intending to create a single federalist republic of the former colonial states, a United States of South America, but having won independence, there are squabbles and rebellions among the armies of liberation. Military and political rivals kill one another. The warfare is non-stop. Bolivar, in his late 40s, is dying and weighing his options—participate in another military campaign, leave for a European retirement and a chance to recover his health, or lend his support to a political solution. Sorrow, not pride of accomplishment, and sometimes anger cloud his memories and his thoughts of his limited future. The prerequisite goal has been accomplished; the end goal seems to be shared by no one but a small coterie of devotees, who in truth seem more loyal to the man than the dream. <br /><br />The novel takes us convincingly into the mind of the General. “’All of us here are exiles,’ said the General. ‘I’ve lived in Venezuela only six years since this began, and the rest of the time has been spent heading off calamities in half the world.’” The General is conversing with a young aide from Mexico, eager to earn his battle spurs. “The General still had not looked him in the face. ‘Well, you chose the wrong destiny,’ he said. ‘The only wars here will be civil wars, and those are like killing your own mother.’” <br /><br />But Garcia Marquez is not content with a brilliantly written work of historical fiction, The General in His Labyrinth is about how age and ambition treat us all, rudely and with full challenge to our dignity and determination. It is the universality of Bolivar’s plight that makes this novel a work of art, the all too human frailty of our bodies and our lives that haunts us after reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11009358052738587997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3650151759547726790">Filling in empty Review Boxes today. Very much enjoyed this one. Perhaps it's to be scored slightly lower than G's two big ones ; maybe third or fourth?; I couldn't quite say. But it does rank highly in that little micro=genre known as the dictator novel -- below <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/349829.I__the_Supreme' title='I, the Supreme by Augusto Roa Bastos' rel='nofollow noopener'>I, the Supreme</a> and (way) above <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53969.The_Feast_of_the_Goat' title='The Feast of the Goat by Mario Vargas Llosa' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Feast of the Goat</a>.   <span id="freeText3650151759547726790" style="display:none">Filling in empty Review Boxes today. Very much enjoyed this one. Perhaps it's to be scored slightly lower than G's two big ones ; maybe third or fourth?; I couldn't quite say. But it does rank highly in that little micro=genre known as the dictator novel -- below <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/349829.I__the_Supreme" title="I, the Supreme by Augusto Roa Bastos" rel="nofollow noopener">I, the Supreme</a> and (way) above <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53969.The_Feast_of_the_Goat" title="The Feast of the Goat by Mario Vargas Llosa" rel="nofollow noopener">The Feast of the Goat</a>. I've got a few more of these dictator novels I want to kick out in the coming periods of time. Generally speaking, GGM is really very good, deserving of his accolades ; but I just can't quite rest til I say something about how much other great stuff and even better stuff lurks south of our border and which goes unread untranslated oop and other such. Nothing against GGM ; just a whole bunch FOR how much there really is if we just scratch a little outside the parameters of our aenemic literary world (NYC or MFA? No thanks!). I mean, really great stuff! Happy days to be a reader! </span>

<div id="review_218723895" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/218723895" />
    <a title="WordsBeyondBorders" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863599-wordsbeyondborders"><img alt="WordsBeyondBorders" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/218723895?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WordsBeyondBorders" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WordsBeyondBorders" href="/user/show/4863599-wordsbeyondborders">WordsBeyondBorders</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer218723895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5389752255533473678">'The General In His Labyrinth' is Marquez's fictional reconstruction of Simon Bolivar's, the liberator of South America from the Spanish, last days. Being an Indian I am not 100% sure of the historical accuracy in the novel, but people expecting a hardcore historical novel may be disappointed as Marquez weaves his own brand of magic interspersing events and actions that you would not expect in a novel of this genre while maintaining the relevance of the genre also. After all, this is a person wh</span>
  <span id="freeText5389752255533473678" style="display:none">'The General In His Labyrinth' is Marquez's fictional reconstruction of Simon Bolivar's, the liberator of South America from the Spanish, last days. Being an Indian I am not 100% sure of the historical accuracy in the novel, but people expecting a hardcore historical novel may be disappointed as Marquez weaves his own brand of magic interspersing events and actions that you would not expect in a novel of this genre while maintaining the relevance of the genre also. After all, this is a person who created an entirely fictional village 'Macondo' and in someways made it the center of the physical Latin American world. So, being an outsider I will just give short introduction to this novel, hoping to persuade those who read this post to go out and grab the novel too :)<br />Simon Bolivar was a one page/paragraph note to me in my ninth standard history class. Just knew him as the liberator of the entire Latin American continent. So generally, in such cases we unconsciously form a mental image of such persons. But all those is shattered here. As the novel begins, Bolivar is person who is seeing his dream of a united Latin America crumbling before his own eyes, his own authority diminished greatly and even hated by many of the countries/people he helped in their liberation. He is also a person who is holding on his last illusion that he may somehow again be accepted by the people and powers that be. But that is not the case and Bolivar finally decides to leave for Europe. The novel then charts his last days through the journey that he undertakes.<br />The Bolivar we come to know through the journey and flashbacks is not a squeaky white person, but a person with his own failings and faults. He is obstinate, a bad loser even in card games, someone who uses cologne so much that opponents even accuse him wasting money on it, maybe even a person who aspired to be the complete dictator for the entire South America and we even think that maybe the people were justified in turning against him. But he is also a leader who is so careless with money and has lost his entire inherited fortune and whatever he has earned. In fact he cannot even afford a horse to travel and rides a mule and one who cannot afford a first class travel in a barge and has to travel third class, this a person who liberated an entire continent. He is now reduced to talking about a mine which he says is his and is expecting money from it. Other than him, no one even knows if it exists or not. As the travel progresses, we get to see a nearly completely broken man, who rants and raves against his enemies but somehow seems to have lost the drive that led him to be such a great general in the first place. He is almost like a petulant child who refuses to accept things even if it is right in front of him, hoping against hope that goods things would happen. He sees the complete destruction of what he built and is powerless to stop it. Persons he exiled are returning to the continent even as he is preparing to leave it. People in a town throw dirt at him and humiliate him. They write slogans on the wall humiliating him. The death of a close associate who is assassinated seems to come as the final blow to him and he fully gives up all will to live. The novel ends with the general having a moment of epiphany which is one of the most evocative passages I have ever read (will give that below).<br />I felt some parallels between the General and Colonel Aureliano Buendía (Hundred years of Solitude) . Both of them start off with noble intentions, get sucked into doing things that themselves fought against in the first place, are nearly forgotten by the same people they helped and are forever doomed to a life in solitude and labyrinth in the midst of all the people surrounding them.<br />The following passage right at the end of then novel itself should be enough for someone to read it. This is my most favorite of all Marquez books, much much more than his more acclaimed works.<br />//He examined the room with the clairvoyance of his last days and for the first time he saw the truth: the final borrowed bed, the pitiful dressing table whose clouded, patient mirror would not reflect his image again, the chipped porcelain washbasin with the water and towel and soap meant for other hands, the heartless speed of the octogonal clock racing toward the ineluctable appointment at seven minutes past one on his final afternoon of December 17. Then he crossed his arms across his chest and began<br />to listen to the radiant voices of the slaves singing the six o' clock Salve in the mills, and through the window he saw the diamond of Venus in the sky that was dying forever, the eternal snows,the new vine whose yellow bellflowers he would not see bloom on the following Saturday in the house closed in mouring, the final brilliance of life that would never, through all eternity be repeated again.//</span>
  <a data-text-id="5389752255533473678" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_351262054" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/351262054" />
    <a title="Errol Orhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8095049-errol-orhan"><img alt="Errol Orhan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/351262054?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Errol Orhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Errol Orhan" href="/user/show/8095049-errol-orhan">Errol Orhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer351262054" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18372687255537949675">Marquez has written quite a few acclaimed masterpieces, and I like to think that The General in His Labyrinth is one of them.<br/><br/>I bought the book when I was on the airport of Budapest. Although I wasn't really intent on buying a book, I stumbled across the penguin version of this book at the airport's bookstore. I had already read Hunderd Years of Solitude and Love in Times of Cholera (and found them truly magical), but I had heard that Marquez had also written some books that had a political setti</span>
  <span id="freeText18372687255537949675" style="display:none">Marquez has written quite a few acclaimed masterpieces, and I like to think that The General in His Labyrinth is one of them.<br /><br />I bought the book when I was on the airport of Budapest. Although I wasn't really intent on buying a book, I stumbled across the penguin version of this book at the airport's bookstore. I had already read Hunderd Years of Solitude and Love in Times of Cholera (and found them truly magical), but I had heard that Marquez had also written some books that had a political setting. I also knew that he had written a book with the title: The General..., but I have to admit that I did not know that the 'General' referred to Simon Bolivar. So, when I read the summary on the back of this book, and saw how unreasonably cheaply it was, I had no option but to buy it instantly. Of course, I have not regretted it.<br /><br />I started reading it immediately, as I waited for my boarding call and savored a Gorilla Magila smoothie (peanut butter, banana, orange, some other ingredient I forgot, and yoghurt), and have not been able to put it down until the sad moment came when the book was finished. Now when I say sad, I really mean sad, I wanted the book to last for at least two hundred pages more. But this is of course not the case.<br /><br />Like Love... and Hundred Years... The General... is about decay and death. And like those two other books, the story as such is grim, but the book as a whole isn't. It gets funny, it gets sad, and it is moving through and through, but there is a sense of magical dignity that seems to surround Marquez' characters right until the moment they die. And after that, there is nothingness. Now, what makes this story so interesting to me, are the three dying Bolivars. <br /><br />First of all, it is the person Simon Bolivar: like all of us a deeply contradictory personality with flaws, quirks, and charms. The way Marquez writes about him is quite admirable. Although it would be easy to portray him as a caricature or a hero, he paints the picture of a frail man clinging to life.<br /><br />The second Bolivar is the general as a relational figure: a man that keeps popping up in anecdotes, a man the influenced people on a personal level, and is mourned over. This makes for a great archive of anecdotes that have been masterfully retold (edited, made-up, whatever you like), by Marquez, and provide for the prosaic backbone of this story. <br /><br />The third Bolivar is the one lost in his labyrinth: once a powerful general, now a feeble man, who is practically stripped of his worldly powers, but keeps clinging on to the possibility of reclaiming the power and realizing his dream. This last Bolivar, the one from the title, is summarized in two sentences:'The General paid no attention to the masterful reply, because he was shaken by the overwhelming revelation that the headlong race between his misfortunes and his dreams was at that moment reaching the finish line. The rest was darkness.' And that Bolivar, that Bolivar embodies the human condition. That Bolivar is us.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18372687255537949675" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_941921814" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/941921814" />
    <a title="Shane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1798222-shane"><img alt="Shane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526065583p2/1798222.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/941921814?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shane" href="/user/show/1798222-shane">Shane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer941921814" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15531238347056591478">There is very little magic realism here from the master of that form.<br/><br/>This is stylized journalism at its best on the last days in the life of Simon Bolivar, the Liberator of America (comprised of only the upper half of South America as we know it today), as he travels the Magdalena River to board a ship to Europe and head off into self-imposed exile, a ship he never boards. His dream for a united country emerging out of the land he liberated from the Spanish is in tatters, for narrow self-enteres</span>
  <span id="freeText15531238347056591478" style="display:none">There is very little magic realism here from the master of that form.<br /><br />This is stylized journalism at its best on the last days in the life of Simon Bolivar, the Liberator of America (comprised of only the upper half of South America as we know it today), as he travels the Magdalena River to board a ship to Europe and head off into self-imposed exile, a ship he never boards. His dream for a united country emerging out of the land he liberated from the Spanish is in tatters, for narrow self-enterests of the rich families in various sectors are agitating to keep it fragmented for posterity.<br /><br />Bolivar is a flawed man, aged beyond his 47 years from the ravages of love and war, a man with boundless mental energy despite his crumbling body, a man who hates to lose even at a game of cards. He never gave of himself to those dear to him, especially to his many lovers, was never injured in the many battles he fought in, always blamed the other for his misfortunes, and wanted sympathy and adulation, despite his claims to the contrary. And yet he was loved by many and reviled by the rest.<br /><br />While this book covered a missing part of my history of South America, and attempted to bring to life a vital historical figure, it did not rise to the level of a novel, for the end was predetermined and the journey was likened to that plodding trip down the Magdalena with no breakwaters at the end to bring it to a roaring climax. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15531238347056591478" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15060563266433390741">A provocative read into the psyche of Bolivar. I feel as though I am hamstrung by my own ignorance of South American history during this period, as well as much of Bolivar's life. <br/><br/>It was fascinating to read into the inner politics between his generals and him. The names really lacked meaning they should have.<br/><br/>All that set aside, Marquez does an admirable job delving into the existential questions that Bolivar faced, or that Bolivar should have been asking himself in the first place. The novel slo</span>
  <span id="freeText15060563266433390741" style="display:none">A provocative read into the psyche of Bolivar. I feel as though I am hamstrung by my own ignorance of South American history during this period, as well as much of Bolivar's life. <br /><br />It was fascinating to read into the inner politics between his generals and him. The names really lacked meaning they should have.<br /><br />All that set aside, Marquez does an admirable job delving into the existential questions that Bolivar faced, or that Bolivar should have been asking himself in the first place. The novel slowly but surely plods -- as slowly as Bolivar must have sailed down that river -- until it reaches a euphoric climax in the final pages. The ending is written with more poetic beauty than I recall from a novel.</span>

  <span id="freeText9837379541718208661" style="display:none">"It was the end. General Simon Jose Antonio de la Santisima Trinidad Bolivar y Palacios was leaving forever. He had wrested from Spanish domination an empire five times more vast than all of Europe, he had led twenty years of wars to keep it free and united, and he had governed it with a firm hand until the week before, but when it was time to leave he did not even take away with him the consolation that anyone believed in his departure. The only man with enough lucidity to know he really was going, and where he was going to, was the English diplomat, who wrote in an official report to his government: "The time he has left will hardly be enough for him to reach his grave."<br /></span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2344163641">
  <td colspan="2">
    <div class="updateActionLinks">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400034703" href="/book/show/876775.The_General_in_His_Labyrinth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76584552-suziey?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3149402315" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16425510789099137105">True Rating: 2.75/5<br/><br/>A romanticized account of General Simón Bolívar's last days as he makes his way through towns he helped liberate. <br/><br/>The writing style threw me off. It blended Bolívar's past with his present. Made it difficult for me to figure out when he was reminiscing. Besides that, there was a lot of focus on the general's womanizing. Who he slept with, when, and where. Blegh. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zZ3NvIk0XNRWkGR2jgbJDeJ1RbLHUfiT95s1WgyqhImDv0UjohlgN789JinfIf/ycU4rHttAcx0u+dHks1XKfw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7YMeUWoRj+lO6qsb8bFxh2MCaUpc0Vnl9DrS3SBzNPejoZbOQTyzCqdH6USglkd48DkH5kDA0mstWDZjn4x6AQ==')})
  }

El libro de arena, a su vez, es una extraordinaria colección de relatos con argumentos imposibles, según su propio autor.\n\nLa literatura borgiana está comprometida con las actividades mentales del hombre contemporáneo y con su reflexión respecto al mundo que le ha tocado aceptar. Aquel Primer Premio<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13311319260204209580\" style=\"display:none\">Libro inaugural en su carrera de escritor, Uno y el Universo es un conjunto de articulos escritos en 1945 y premiados por un jurado de reconocido merito: Adolfo Bioy Casares, Vicente Barbieri, Leonidas Barletta y Ricardo Molinari. Aquel Primer Premio en Prosa de la Municipalidad de la Ciudad de Buenos Aires presentaba a un Ernesto Sabato joven y punzante, lucido y reflexivo.Los textos que aparecen en este historico volumen pasan revista a los hechos politicos y filosoficos heredados del siglo XIX y cuyas consecuencias tienen todavia hoy profundas resonancias en la conformacion de sujeto y su relacion con el mundo de las ideas, las ilusiones y el progreso. &quot;La ciencia es una escuela de modestia, de valor intelectual y de tolerancia: muestra que el pensamiento es un proceso, que no hay gran hombre que no se haya equivocado, que no hay dogma que no se haya desmoronado ante el embate de los nuevos hechos.&quot; Con la agudeza que luego afianzara, pero con la misma conviccion moral de siempre, Sabato ilustra su permanente interrogacion sobre el hombre, censura la moral neutral de la ciencia y aboga por una cultura donde tengan cabida los otros, en una nueva imagen del mundo.&quot;Habitante sucesivo de la ciencia, la ficcion y la etica&quot; -como dijera la poeta Maria Negroni-, el nombre Sabato ha desafiado con su obra y su pensamiento todas las limitaciones del sujeto, ha iluminado con su predica y sigue ejerciendo la mejor docencia: la que ensena a creer en un mundo mejor.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13311319260204209580\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13450.Gabriel_Garc_a_M_rquez">About Gabriel García Márquez</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/13450.Gabriel_Garc_a_M_rquez"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1588856705p3/13450.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/13450.Gabriel_Garc_a_M_rquez">Gabriel García Márquez
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
29,389
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2095565787950521005">Gabriel José de la Concordia Garcí­a Márquez was a Colombian novelist, short-story writer, screenwriter and journalist. Garcí­a Márquez, familiarly known as "Gabo" in his native country, was considered one of the most significant authors of the 20th century. In 1982, he was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature.<br/><br/>He studied at the University of Bogotá and later worked as a reporter for the Colombian</span>
  <span id="freeText2095565787950521005" style="display:none">Gabriel José de la Concordia Garcí­a Márquez was a Colombian novelist, short-story writer, screenwriter and journalist. Garcí­a Márquez, familiarly known as "Gabo" in his native country, was considered one of the most significant authors of the 20th century. In 1982, he was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature.<br /><br />He studied at the University of Bogotá and later worked as a reporter for the Colombian newspaper <i>El Espectador</i> and as a foreign correspondent in Rome, Paris, Barcelona, Caracas, and New York. He wrote many acclaimed non-fiction works and short stories, but is best-known for his novels, such as <i>One Hundred Years of Solitude</i> (1967) and <i>Love in the Time of Cholera</i> (1985). His works have achieved significant critical acclaim and widespread commercial success, most notably for popularizing a literary style labeled as magical realism, which uses magical elements and events in order to explain real experiences. Some of his works are set in a fictional village called Macondo, and most of them express the theme of solitude.<br /><br />Having previously written shorter fiction and screenplays, García Márquez sequestered himself away in his Mexico City home for an extended period of time to complete his novel <i>Cien años de soledad</i>, or <i>One Hundred Years of Solitude</i>, published in 1967. The author drew international acclaim for the work, which ultimately sold tens of millions of copies worldwide. García Márquez is credited with helping introduce an array of readers to magical realism, a genre that combines more conventional storytelling forms with vivid, layers of fantasy.<br /><br />Another one of his novels, <i>El amor en los tiempos del cólera</i> (1985), or <i>Love in the Time of Cholera</i>, drew a large global audience as well. The work was partially based on his parents' courtship and was adapted into a 2007 film starring Javier Bardem. García Márquez wrote seven novels during his life, with additional titles that include <i>El general en su laberinto</i> (1989), or <i>The General in His Labyrinth</i>, and <i>Del amor y otros demonios</i> (1994), or <i>Of Love and Other Demons</i>.<br /><br />(Arabic: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5029628._" rel="nofollow noopener">جابرييل جارسيا ماركيز</a>) (Hebrew: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7091051._" rel="nofollow noopener">גבריאל גארסיה מרקס</a>) </span>
  <a data-text-id="2095565787950521005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/1066730-el-general-en-su-laberinto">Trivia About The General in Hi...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
